May 30

Architected five-level learning curriculum structured like Anthropic's educational approach
Architected five-level learning curriculum structured like Anthropic's educational approach
Good call. Before we write a single term, let's get the architecture right — because a good knowledge base isn't just a long glossary. Let me think through this properly.

The thing that makes Anthropic's content different
Anthropic doesn't publish a dictionary. They publish educational content organized around understanding — you can feel the hand of someone who knows the reader, knows where they're confused, and is walking them from where they are to where they need to be. Their guides have a direction. The BytePlus glossary has entries. That's the gap we exploit.

Our edge: organize by journey, not alphabet.

Nobody needs the 38th A-to-Z AI glossary. But everyone needs a clear answer to: "What do I actually need to understand, and in what order?" That's what Anthropic would build. That's what we build.

The structure: five levels, one curriculum
Instead of A–Z, the knowledge base is a learning path — five stages from zero to operating AI agents. Each level is a coherent unit you could read on its own, sell as a standalone booklet, or walk a client through in a coaching session.

Level 1 — Zero to Aware (~20 terms) For anyone who's been nodding along in meetings about AI and wants to stop pretending. The bedrock: neural network, training, model, inference, overfitting, classification, clustering, dataset, features, labels, supervised vs unsupervised. These are the words everyone uses and almost nobody can actually define.

Level 2 — Understanding LLMs (~20 terms) What changed everything and why. LLM, tokenization, transformer & attention, pre-training, fine-tuning, embedding, context window, temperature, hallucination, alignment, RLHF, quantization. Understanding why a language model behaves the way it does.

Level 3 — Using Models Well (~20 terms) Practical literacy for the person who's already using AI but wants results, not luck. Prompt engineering, system prompt, chain-of-thought, few-shot, RAG, vector database, semantic search, function calling, grounding, structured output, evaluation, guardrails.

Level 4 — Building Agents (~20 terms) The shift from typing prompts to building things that act. AI agent, agentic loop, orchestration, planning, memory (short + long-term), tool use, human-in-the-loop, task decomposition, multi-agent systems, stop conditions, error recovery, the ReAct pattern.

Level 5 — The Harness (~20 terms) Your thesis made concrete. Harness architecture, recursive learning, episodic memory, safeguard design, harness evolution, AI observability, prompt management, vibe coding, AI pipelines, the digital operator. This is where the knowledge base becomes your knowledge base — nobody else ends their glossary here.

Total: ~100 well-chosen terms. Enough to be comprehensive; tight enough to actually finish.

The term format (already proven)
We tested the card structure in the PDF and it holds up. Stick with it:

Term + category pill
One-liner — the definition in a single sentence, clay accent
Plain English — 3–5 sentences, no jargon, factually tight
In plain terms — an original analogy in the serif callout box
Why it matters — one sentence grounding it in the real world
(New addition) Related terms — 2–3 cross-links so readers navigate, not just read linearly
One design addition: a level indicator in small mono on each card (L1 / L2 etc.) so readers know where they are in the journey and can jump in at the right level.

Deliverable formats — three things, built from one data file
The smart move is to maintain the content as a single structured data file (a Python dict or JSON) and build every format from it. That way adding a term once propagates everywhere.

1. The website — a searchable, filterable knowledge base section on leanprogressiq.com. Each term expands inline or links to its own page. Filterable by level and category. Free to access — this is the SEO and trust-building play. Anthropic's free educational content is what builds their authority.

2. PDF booklets — one per level, sold individually on Ko-fi or Payhip (e.g. €3–5 each), or bundled as the complete guide. Level 5 ("The Harness") becomes the flagship paid piece — it's the one nobody else has.

3. Social cards — each term as a single clean visual: the clay block, the term large in Fraunces, the analogy in the serif callout. One card = one TikTok or YouTube Short, one post, one piece of content. A hundred terms = a hundred pieces of content from one data file.

Production pipeline
This is how we stay sane and actually finish it:

Write in batches of 10–15 terms per session, building level by level. Each session: I draft the terms, you review and adjust the wording (especially the analogies — you know your audience's level), then we run the build script and the new terms appear in all three formats. No rewriting, no reformatting, no duplication.

The data file is the master. The build script handles the rest.

Architected structured curriculum pivoting from reference to practical lessons
Right. Not a reference. A course — something with a learning arc, practical exercises, and a clear outcome at the end. Let me think through that properly.

What makes learning material different from a knowledge base
A knowledge base answers "what is X?" Learning material answers "can you do X yet?"

Every unit needs a learner objective — a verb, not a noun. Not "backpropagation" but "you can explain why a model needs training data and what happens when it has too little." The test isn't recognition, it's application.

Your instinct as a language trainer is exactly right here. You don't teach vocabulary lists. You teach communication competencies — by level, in context, with practice. Same architecture applies.

The course — working title: Harness Ready
From AI user to AI operator — in five modules.

Target learner
A working professional — European, likely non-technical — who uses ChatGPT occasionally and vaguely senses their job is changing. They want to go from "I know AI is important" to "I actually know what I'm doing and nobody can catch me off guard in a meeting." Your Learnlight / Bibo client, essentially.

The five modules
Module 1 — The Shift (orientation, 3 units) Not technical. Sets the frame. Why this wave is different, what the three literacy levels are, and an honest map of what changes in which jobs and when.

Unit	Objective
1.1 The wave	You can explain in your own words why AI literacy is now a professional requirement, not optional.
1.2 Three levels	You can place yourself on the literacy ladder and name the one level that protects your career.
1.3 The jobs map	You can identify which parts of your own role are most and least at risk — and why.
Module 2 — How the Machine Thinks (conceptual foundation, 4 units) Enough technical understanding to stop being surprised. Not deep — wide. How an LLM works, why it makes things up, what it's actually doing when it responds.

Unit	Objective
2.1 What an LLM actually is	You can explain the predict-the-next-token idea to a colleague in two sentences.
2.2 Why it sounds confident and wrong	You can describe what hallucination is and name two situations where you'd verify the output.
2.3 Memory, context and forgetting	You can explain why a model "forgets" and what that means for how you use it.
2.4 Tokens, cost and speed	You can read a pricing page and understand why longer conversations cost more.
Module 3 — Using Models Well (applied skill, 4 units) The practical gap — most people use AI like Google. This module teaches them to use it like a collaborator. Heavy on exercises.

Unit	Objective
3.1 What a good prompt actually does	You can rewrite a vague prompt into a specific one and predict why the output improves.
3.2 Getting reliable answers	You can use chain-of-thought and structured output techniques to get consistently useful results.
3.3 Working with documents	You can use a model to analyze, summarize and extract from a real document in your work.
3.4 Knowing when not to trust it	You can apply a simple verification checklist before acting on model output.
Module 4 — The Agent and the Harness (conceptual + applied, 4 units) The frontier module. This is what almost nobody teaches. What an agent actually is, what the harness does, and how to recognize a well-built one versus a dangerous one.

Unit	Objective
4.1 From chatbot to agent	You can describe in plain terms the difference between a single prompt and an agentic loop.
4.2 The five parts of a harness	You can name planning, memory, tools, safeguards and recursive learning — and give a real example of each.
4.3 Safeguards and why they exist	You can explain what a safeguard is, why it's non-optional, and identify one missing in a scenario.
4.4 Recognizing a good harness	You can evaluate a described agent workflow and identify its weakest point.
Module 5 — Building Your AI Stack (personal application, 3 units) The outcome module. Learner leaves with something real — a mapped workflow, a personal AI toolkit, a plan for staying current.

Unit	Objective
5.1 Map your own work	You can identify three tasks in your current job that an agent could handle — and one that needs a human.
5.2 Your first workflow	You can describe (and ideally run) a simple multi-step workflow using tools you already have.
5.3 Staying current	You can name the two or three sources that will keep you ahead — and explain why most AI news is noise.
Total: 18 units across 5 modules. Each unit ~20–30 minutes of active learning.

Each unit's structure — consistent, every time
Borrowed directly from your language lesson architecture:

CONTEXT      — Why this matters, right now. 1 paragraph, punchy.
              (Same as the "kicker" on the site — urgency first.)

CONCEPT      — The idea, explained plainly with an original analogy.
              (Same format as the Field Guide cards.)

SEE IT       — A real scenario or worked example. Concrete.
              (Not "imagine you are a company." Specific and grounded.)

YOUR TURN    — A single practical exercise. Doable in 5–10 minutes.
              (Can be reflection, a rewrite task, a checklist, a mini-audit.)

TAKE AWAY    — One sentence. What you now know how to do.
              (The learner objective restated as an achievement.)
Format — three outputs from one content file
Same principle as the knowledge base: write once, publish everywhere.

A — PDF workbook (the sellable product) One PDF per module, designed in the clay aesthetic. Each unit gets 2–3 pages: concept card, worked example, exercise with space to write. Sold individually (€5–8) or as the full course bundle (€25–30). Ko-fi or Payhip. Goes to coaching clients as premium course material.

B — Web interactive version (the free lead-generator) Module 1 free on the site. Expandable units, exercises that can be typed and saved locally. Converts to paid PDF. SEO play — "AI literacy course free" is findable; "what is a harness" is findable.

C — Lesson script (for your own coaching delivery) You already run 1-on-1 and corporate sessions. Each unit script is a ready-to-run 20-minute coached lesson. The PDF workbook is the homework; the session is the debrief. This is your Talaera pitch, your Speexx differentiator — you have a curriculum.

The one decision
Who is the first learner — and what is their job?

Everything else is locked in. The only variable is the protagonist of the "SEE IT" examples and exercises. If the primary learner is a European professional in a corporate role (finance, telecom, pharma — your Learnlight world), the examples are email responses, reports, client briefings, data analysis. If it's a general working adult (broader, Ko-fi audience), examples stay general. If it's coaches and trainers like you (B2B play — sell the curriculum to language schools and corporate L&D), the examples are lesson planning, client prep, session reporting.

You could aim at all three eventually. But the first version needs one clear voice in the "SEE IT" sections.

HARNESS READY
Module 1 — The Shift
Three units · 60–90 minutes total

By the end of this module you will be able to:

Explain in your own words why this moment is different from previous technology waves
Place yourself on the AI literacy ladder and name the level that protects your career
Identify which parts of your own job are most and least exposed — and what to do about it
Unit 1.1 — The Wave
Objective: You can explain why AI literacy is now a professional requirement, not optional.

Context
Every generation of working people has had one moment where a new technology quietly redrew the rules — and the people who noticed early enough had a very different decade than the ones who waited.

In the 1980s, it was the personal computer. People who learned to use it became faster, more capable, harder to replace. People who dismissed it became dependent on those who hadn't. In the late 1990s, it was the internet. In both cases, the people who got left behind weren't less intelligent or less hardworking. They just made the same mistake: they assumed the new thing was optional for longer than it was.

This is that moment again — except it is moving faster, it applies to more jobs, and the gap between "I'll get to it" and "I'm already behind" is measured in months, not years.

You are reading this because you already sense that. This unit is about replacing that vague sense of urgency with something more useful: clarity.

Concept — Tool or Shift?
There is an important difference between a tool and a shift.

A tool is something you add to your workflow. A calculator is a tool. So is a spreadsheet, a smartphone, a search engine. You learn it, it speeds you up, life goes on.

A shift changes the workflow itself. Email wasn't just a faster fax machine — it changed how quickly people expected decisions, what counted as a normal working day, who could reach whom and when. The fax machine was a tool. Email was a shift. People who treated it as just another tool to get around to eventually found themselves permanently in reaction mode.

AI is a shift. The question is not whether you use it. The question is when you can no longer afford not to.

Three things make this shift different from the ones before:

1. It is general purpose. Previous technology waves disrupted specific industries or specific types of work. The internet transformed media, retail, communication. But a factory worker in the 1990s could reasonably conclude it wasn't their problem yet. AI applies to almost every knowledge job — writing, analysis, communication, planning, teaching, coding, diagnosing, advising. There is no professional corner where it isn't already arriving.

2. The adoption gap is compressing. The time between "early adopters are using this" and "everyone is expected to use this" used to be measured in years. With AI tools, it is measured in months. By the time it feels normal to your industry, the advantage of understanding it will already belong to someone else.

3. It is already in your workplace, whether you are using it or not. Your colleagues are experimenting. Your competitors are deploying. Your manager may be watching. The people who stay visible in this environment are not the ones who did the most — they are the ones who most clearly understand what they are doing and why.

See It
Marta, logistics coordinator, Hamburg Marta has worked in freight forwarding for nine years. Six months ago, her manager started using an AI tool to write the weekly exception reports — the summaries of delays, customs issues and routing changes she used to spend Friday afternoons compiling. What used to take her two hours now takes him twenty minutes.

She has not been asked to do it since. She isn't sure if that's a good thing or a warning.

Tomás, sales representative, Warsaw Tomás noticed two months ago that two of his colleagues are turning around client presentations at roughly twice the speed they used to. He has asked indirectly but hasn't got a clear answer about what they're doing differently. He's started to feel a low-level anxiety that he can't quite name. He hasn't done anything about it yet, mostly because he doesn't know where to start.

Isabelle, secondary school teacher, Lyon Isabelle was told at a staff meeting that her school is "exploring the use of AI in teaching." No further details were given. She is curious about what it means for her lesson planning, and quietly worried about whether it will be used to monitor her somehow. She has downloaded one AI app, used it twice, and hasn't gone back.

What these three people have in common: they are not panicking. They are not Luddites. They are simply at the beginning of a learning curve they haven't started yet — and they can feel the gradient under their feet.

This course is the starting point.

Your Turn
Take a blank page or open a notes app. Write freely — don't edit yourself.

Question 1: Where has AI already appeared in your professional life, even indirectly? (A tool your company is trialling, a task that's been automated, a colleague who works differently, a client who expects something new. Anything. Even "I've heard it mentioned in meetings and felt like I was missing something" counts.)

Question 2: What have you avoided learning about AI so far — and why? (Too technical? Too uncertain? Not enough time? Didn't feel relevant yet?) Be honest. This is a baseline, not a test.

Keep your answers. You will come back to them at the end of the course, and the distance between where you started and where you finish will be clearer for having written it down now.

Time: 5–10 minutes.

Take Away
You can describe, in a sentence, why this moment is different from the last time technology changed your work — and name one place AI has already touched your professional life, even at a distance.

Unit 1.2 — Three Levels
Objective: You can place yourself on the AI literacy ladder and name the level that protects your career.

Context
Most AI advice sits at one of two extremes. Either it is basic to the point of uselessness ("use ChatGPT to save time!") or it is technical to the point of being written for someone who already has a computer science degree. The enormous gap in the middle — the level that actually matters for most working people — is almost never taught.

That gap is where your career lives. This unit gives you a map of it.

Concept — The Three Levels
Level 1 — User literacy

You can use AI tools to get things done. You know how to write a reasonable prompt, you've used a chatbot for drafting or research, you can get useful output without too much trial and error.

This is where most people who are "using AI" currently sit. It is necessary — you cannot skip this level — but it is not a professional advantage for much longer. Within eighteen months, user literacy will be the minimum expected, the same way basic computer skills stopped being a differentiator twenty years ago.

Level 2 — Directional literacy

You understand why the model behaves the way it does. Why it occasionally states things that are completely wrong. Why the same prompt gives different results on different days. Why longer context costs more. How to structure a task so the model handles it reliably. How to catch its mistakes before they become your problem.

At this level, you are not just using AI — you are directing it. You trust it in the right places and question it in the right places. This is the level most people never reach, and where the real gap opens up between people who use AI and people who get results from it.

Level 3 — Operational literacy

You can build systems that use AI — workflows, agents, harnesses — that work reliably without you supervising every single step. You understand what an agent is, what a harness does, how memory and safeguards work. You can commission these systems, evaluate whether they are safe and reliable, and use them to multiply what one person can produce.

You do not need to be a programmer to reach Level 3. That is one of the things this course exists to demonstrate. By the end of it, you will be here.

The key insight — and it is worth sitting with for a moment — is that moving from Level 1 to Level 3 is not a matter of technical knowledge. It is a matter of understanding. The same way that understanding why a client negotiates the way they do makes you better at negotiation, without needing to become a psychologist — understanding why an AI model behaves the way it does makes you dramatically better at working with it, without needing to become an engineer.

See It
Three people at the same insurance company, using the same AI tool, on the same Tuesday afternoon.

Person A uses the tool to draft response letters to customer queries. She pastes the query, asks for a response, and sends it after a quick read. When it gets facts wrong about a policy clause, she catches it sometimes and misses it sometimes. She saves roughly 30 minutes a day. She is at Level 1.

Person B uses the same tool for the same task, but she has taken the time to understand that the model makes things up when it doesn't know — and that it is most likely to do so with specific numbers, dates, and technical clauses. She always checks those specifically. She also writes much clearer prompts that specify the tone, the length, and the single thing the letter needs to accomplish. She handles more complex cases than Person A because she is not afraid of where the tool fails — she knows how to manage it. She saves closer to two hours a day. She is at Level 2.

Person C noticed six months ago that Person B was faster than everyone else. He asked her what she was doing differently. He took that understanding and built a simple workflow: new queries come in, the relevant policy section is pulled automatically and included in the prompt, a draft response is generated with the specific policy reference pre-filled. He reviews and approves instead of drafting. He handles roughly three times the caseload he used to. His manager has started asking him to show others how he works. He is at Level 3.

Same tool. Same company. Three completely different outcomes — not because of talent, seniority or technical skill. Because of clarity about what the tool is actually doing.

Your Turn
Look at the three levels again. Read each description carefully.

Step 1: Mark honestly where you are right now. Not where you want to be, not where you think you should be — where you actually are on a regular working day.

Step 2: Write one sentence that completes this: "If I were operating at Level [the next level], my Tuesday afternoon would look different in this specific way: ___________."

Be concrete. Not "I would be more productive." What would actually change — which task, which outcome, which conversation?

Time: 5–10 minutes.

Take Away
You know which of the three levels describes you right now — and you can name one concrete thing that reaching the next level would change about a specific day in your working life.

Unit 1.3 — The Jobs Map
Objective: You can identify which parts of your own role are most and least exposed to AI — and what that means for where to focus.

Context
The question that keeps people up at night is the wrong question. "Will AI take my job?" is too big and too vague to do anything useful with. It produces either false reassurance ("not my field") or paralyzing anxiety ("everything is at risk"), neither of which leads anywhere.

The right question is smaller and sharper: Which of my specific tasks are changing — and what fills the space?

That question is answerable. And once you answer it, you have a map instead of a fog.

Concept — Tasks, Not Jobs
AI does not replace roles. It replaces tasks within roles. The distinction matters.

A doctor's role involves diagnosing, communicating with patients, making judgment calls under uncertainty, staying current with research, managing a relationship of trust across years. The task of reading a scan and flagging anomalies — that specific, bounded thing — is being done faster and in some cases more accurately by AI already. The role of doctor is not disappearing. The task of reading scans is changing. The doctor who understands this can treat it as a gift of time. The one who doesn't may feel like the ground is moving under them for no clear reason.

Here is the framework that maps almost any knowledge job:

Category 1 — Routine and language-based Writing, summarizing, formatting, translating, categorizing, drafting, transcribing, scheduling, compiling. These tasks follow patterns, involve language, and have outputs that can be evaluated by reading them. High exposure. AI handles these tasks reasonably well already, and better every month.

Category 2 — Judgment under uncertainty Deciding when information is incomplete. Advising when the right answer depends on context, relationship, or stakes that a machine can't fully read. Managing conflict. Building trust. Taking accountability for an outcome. Lower exposure. AI can assist — it can generate options, surface risks, draft arguments — but it cannot be the one who owns the decision. Not yet. Probably not soon.

Category 3 — Creating and building new things Identifying a problem nobody has named. Designing something that hasn't existed before in quite this form. Combining ideas across domains in an unexpected way. Noticing what's missing. Also lower exposure — and this is the area where the people who understand AI become dramatically more productive, because the tool amplifies original thinking rather than replacing it.

The people who struggle in the next ten years are not those whose routine tasks get automated — that will be most of us, at some point, in some part of our work. The ones who struggle are those who are not ready to move up to the task that replaces the one that changed.

The move is almost always from Category 1 toward Categories 2 and 3. Not because Category 1 disappears — but because the time it used to take is now available for something more valuable.

See It
Fatou, financial analyst, Dakar (working for a European firm) Fatou's week: roughly 50% Category 1 — compiling data into reports, writing market summaries, formatting presentations for clients. 40% Category 2 — advising clients on portfolio decisions, interpreting results in context, handling client anxiety during volatile periods. 10% Category 3 — occasionally spotting a pattern across client data that nobody's looked at before.

The 50% is where her employer's AI tools are already making inroads. Fatou has noticed that the reports take her less time when she uses the tools available — but she hasn't been asked to do less of them. The expectation has crept up: more analysis, same hours. The question for Fatou is not whether her job is safe. It is whether the time freed by faster reporting gets redirected toward the 40% where she is genuinely irreplaceable — or whether it just fills with more Category 1 volume.

Kenji, physical therapist, Osaka Kenji's week: 15% Category 1 — patient notes, session reports, insurance documentation. 70% Category 2 — assessing each patient's progress, adjusting treatment in response to what he sees, reading the signals that a patient is at risk of re-injury, managing the relationship that keeps them coming back. 15% Category 3 — occasionally designing a new rehab protocol for an unusual case.

The documentation (15%) is already being handled faster by voice-to-text AI transcription, which his practice adopted last year. He now has roughly four extra hours a week. He has used most of them to take on two additional patients. He hasn't changed how he thinks about his work, but the shape of his week has shifted — less typing, more treating. He is accidentally becoming more of what he was trained to be.

Priya, customer service team lead, Bangalore (managing a European client account) Priya manages a team of twelve handling first-response customer queries. Last year, 30% of incoming tickets were automated by an AI system. Her team now handles the cases the AI escalates — the complex, the ambiguous, the emotionally charged. Her job has changed significantly: less "manage the queue," more "manage the system that manages the queue" and "handle the cases the system gets wrong."

Priya is, without knowing the vocabulary for it, already doing Level 3 work. She is the human in the loop. She is the one who evaluates whether the AI's escalations are correct, identifies the patterns in what it gets wrong, and feeds that back to the team that maintains it. She does not feel like she has a more valuable job. She should.

Your Turn
Take a blank page and draw three columns. Label them:

Routine & Language	Judgment	Creating & Building
Spend 5–10 minutes putting your typical work tasks into the columns. Use your own words, your actual tasks — don't try to match the examples above. A few that come to mind are enough; you don't need to be exhaustive.

Then answer these two questions in writing:

Which column currently takes the most of your working time?
Which column do people value you most for — where do you get the feedback, the thanks, the requests for your specific input?
If your answers to those two questions are different columns, you have just identified exactly where to direct your energy in the next year.

Time: 10–15 minutes.

Take Away
You can sort your own work tasks across the three categories — and name the one task type that deserves more of your attention as AI continues to develop.

Module 1 — Summary
You have covered the ground that most people never cover explicitly — they feel the shift but can't name it, they sense they're at the wrong level but can't locate themselves, they worry about their job but ask the wrong question.

You now have:

A clear description of why this moment is different and why "optional" is running out of time
A map of the three literacy levels and an honest sense of where you stand
A framework for thinking about your own work that turns vague anxiety into something specific and actionable
Module 2 is where we get inside the machine. Not deep — you won't need maths or code. But you will come out of it understanding why an LLM behaves the way it does, why it sometimes sounds completely sure about something completely wrong, and what "context" and "memory" actually mean. Once you understand that, you can stop being surprised by AI and start directing it.

That's when the real work starts.

Harness Ready — the course. A structured 5-module learning path from AI user to AI operator. The thing you teach. This exists independent of who Dušan is — a nurse in Osaka and a sales rep in Warsaw both need it, and neither of them needs to know your biography to benefit from it.

Ruby — the platform. The place where learners actually go. The course lives here, the tools live here, the experience lives here. This is what you're building when you say "separate web presentation."

The relationship is simple and important:

Lean Progress IQ built Ruby. Ruby teaches what Lean Progress IQ knows.

One is the craftsperson. One is the product. They point to each other — Ruby's about page says "built by Dušan at Lean Progress IQ"; Lean Progress IQ's site says "I built Ruby to teach what I know." But they're distinct. That distinction matters for three reasons: Ruby can grow beyond you, Ruby can be sold as a product, and Ruby can eventually be white-labeled for corporate clients without your personal story getting in the way.

What Ruby is

The tools — this is the key insight
You said "offer some tools to our learners." The instinct is right but the framing needs to be sharper. The tools aren't extras you offer alongside the course. They are the learning. Every tool demonstrates a concept from a unit — you encounter the idea in the reading, then you experience it in the tool. That's what makes this different from every other AI course, which is mostly slides and text.

Here's the map — each tool connects to a specific unit:

Tool 1 — The Literacy Locator (Module 1, Unit 1.2) A 10-question interactive assessment. Answers questions about how you currently work with AI, what you understand about it, what you can do. Output: your level (1, 2 or 3), a plain-English explanation of what that means, and your recommended entry point in the course. This is the free entry tool — no signup needed, runs in two minutes, shows value before you've paid anything. Every marketing touchpoint ends here.

Tool 2 — The Jobs Map (Module 1, Unit 1.3) The interactive version of the three-column exercise from the workbook. Enter your job title; Ruby suggests tasks across the three categories; you edit and confirm. Output: a visual exposure map of your role, downloadable. Personalizes the course before it starts.

Tool 3 — The Context Window (Module 2, Unit 2.3) A visual demo: a conversation filling up in real time, token by token, until something falls off the edge. You see and feel what "forgetting" means before you read the explanation. Tactile, memorable, impossible to explain with words alone.

Tool 4 — Hallucination Hunter (Module 2, Unit 2.2) A short game: the AI makes five statements about a topic you choose — some accurate, some fabricated with total confidence. You identify the ones you'd verify. Calibrates your trust in real time instead of in theory.

Tool 5 — The Prompt Lab (Module 3) Side-by-side: write a vague prompt on the left, see a live critique with specific rewrites suggested, run both and compare outputs. Teaches prompt craft by doing, not by reading about it.

Tool 6 — The Harness Builder (Module 4) A visual canvas — the five harness components (Planning, Memory, Tools, Safeguards, Recursive Learning) as connectable nodes. Given a scenario ("automate my weekly report"), you wire up the harness. It shows you what's missing and why it would fail. This is Hermes Workflows, simplified and turned into a learning experience. The same idea that powers your AgentMesh work, made accessible to a nurse in Belgrade.

Tool 7 — Ask Ruby (throughout) A persistent learning agent available on every page of the platform. Knows the course content. Can explain any concept differently, give examples in your industry, answer "but what does this mean for me specifically." This is the Claude API agent you've already built — repositioned as a tutor instead of a site agent. The demo becomes the product.

The math moments — placed correctly
Five moments in the course where a short equation makes the concept permanently clear. Each is optional, visually separated, and introduced as: "This is the whole equation. Here's what each part means."

Moment	Location	The equation	What it illuminates
Cosine similarity	Unit 2.2	cos θ = (A·B)/(‖A‖‖B‖)	Why embeddings measure meaning as direction
Attention scores	Unit 2.3	softmax(QK<sup>T</sup>/√d)V	How every word weighs every other word
Cross-entropy loss	Unit 3.1	L = −log p(correct)	How training measures "how wrong" in a single number
The RL reward loop	Unit 3.3	r → V(s) → π update	How RLHF teaches a model to prefer better answers
Cosine distance in RAG	Unit 4.3	sim(q, d) = q·d/(‖q‖‖d‖)	Why semantic search finds meaning not keywords
These aren't exams. They're the moment of clarity after the concept — "see, that's all it is."

Module 2 opens with the most important single idea in the course:

A language model isn't searching a database. It isn't reasoning from rules. It learned, from reading an enormous fraction of everything humans have written, what probably comes next. And doing that incredibly well, at enormous scale, produces something that looks a lot like intelligence.

That's the conceptual unlock. Everything else — attention, training, hallucination, harnesses — is an extension of that one idea understood properly.

May 31

Architected phased Node.js CLI agent with Tauri desktop vision
Architected phased Node.js CLI agent with Tauri desktop vision
Good. This is one of the most buildable things on your list — and given you already have AgentMesh running and understand harness architecture from the inside, you're closer than most people who try this.

Let me plan it properly before we write a single line.

What it actually is
Claude Code is, at its core, a loop. That's the whole thing.

Task → Read context → Plan → Execute tools → Observe result → Loop until done
Every "magic" moment — the file it knew to edit, the test it ran to verify, the refactor it split into steps — is that loop running, with a model that's good at coding, given the right tools. There's no secret. The secret is implementing the loop reliably, safely, and with good context management.

Your version is called Ruby Code. It connects directly to the Ruby Diamond vision from your infographics.

Tech stack — decided
No debate. Here's why each choice.

Language: TypeScript (Node.js) You know JS well. The Anthropic SDK is best-in-class in TypeScript. And when you build the Tauri desktop shell in Phase 3, the agent runs as a Node sidecar — same language as your React frontend. Python would be fine for Phase 1 but creates a language split later.

Model: Claude API — Sonnet for speed, Opus for complex refactors You're building on Anthropic's API anyway. Sonnet 4 handles most coding tasks in under 10 seconds per step. Opus for the multi-file architectural changes.

Tool execution: Anthropic native tool use Not regex-parsed XML, not function calling hacks — the proper tools array in the messages API. Clean, typed, reliable.

File editing: targeted diffs, not full rewrites The biggest mistake every first coding agent makes: rewriting the entire file instead of editing the specific block. Full rewrites destroy comments, break formatting, lose context. Diffs are harder to implement but 10x more reliable.

Phase 1 interface: CLI (terminal) Same as Claude Code. Fast to build, fast to test, no UI to debug while the agent itself is broken. Web UI comes in Phase 2.

Architecture
ruby-code/
├── src/
│   ├── agent/
│   │   ├── loop.ts          ← The heart. Plan → act → observe → repeat
│   │   ├── planner.ts       ← Decompose task → ordered steps
│   │   ├── executor.ts      ← Run a tool, return result
│   │   └── context.ts       ← Build & manage project context

│   ├── tools/               ← Everything the agent can do

│   │   ├── read-file.ts

│   │   ├── list-dir.ts
│   │   ├── search-code.ts   ← Grep/ripgrep wrapper
│   │   ├── run-shell.ts     ← Run commands with permission check
│   │   ├── run-tests.ts     ← npm test / pytest / etc.
│   │   └── git.ts           ← Status, diff, staged changes
│   │

The agent loop — the real code pattern
This is what everything else hangs on. The loop is simple. Getting it robust is where the work is.

typescript
async function agentLoop(task: string, context: ProjectContext) {
  const messages: Message[] = [];

  // Build system prompt with project context
  const system = buildSystemPrompt(context);

  // First turn: understand + plan
  messages.push({ role: "user", content: task });

  while (true) {
    const response = await claude.messages.create({
      model: "claude-sonnet-4-20250514",
      max_tokens: 8096,
      system,
      tools: TOOL_SCHEMAS,
      messages,
    });

    // Add assistant response to history
    messages.push({ role: "assistant", content: response.content });

    // If no tool calls → agent is done
    if (response.stop_reason === "end_turn") {
      return extractFinalAnswer(response.content);
    }

    // Execute all tool calls in this response
    const toolResults: ToolResult[] = [];

    for (const block of response.content) {
      if (block.type !== "tool_use") continue;

      // Safety check before execution
      const permitted = await checkPermission(block.name, block.input);
      if (!permitted) {
        toolResults.push({ id: block.id, error: "Blocked by safety policy" });
        continue;
      }

      // Execute the tool
      const result = await executeTool(block.name, block.input);
      display.toolCall(block.name, block.input, result); // show user what happened

      toolResults.push({ id: block.id, content: result });
    }

    // Feed results back → next iteration
    messages.push({
      role: "user",
      content: toolResults.map(r => ({
        type: "tool_result",
        tool_use_id: r.id,
        content: r.content ?? r.error,
      })),
    });
  }
}
That's the whole loop. Around it, you build: better context loading, smarter tool implementations, safety checks, session memory, and finally the UI.

Tools — what the agent can do
Tier 1 — Read only (always permitted)

Tool	What it does	Why it matters
read_file	Read any file with optional line range	Core — agent needs to read before it edits
list_dir	Recursive directory listing	Understand project structure
search_code	Ripgrep across codebase — regex, literal, file type filter	Find the function/class/usage before editing
git_status	Modified files, staged changes, recent commits	Understand current state
git_diff	Show specific file diffs	See what's already changed
read_tests	Find and read test files for a given source file	Know what to verify against
Tier 2 — Normal (permitted by default, shown to user)

Tool	What it does	Key implementation detail
edit_file	Targeted patch: find block → replace block	Use line anchors + surrounding context, NOT full rewrite
write_file	Create new file with content	Safe for new files, requires confirmation on overwrites
run_tests	Execute test suite for a specific file or scope	Capped timeout (30s), captures stdout/stderr
run_shell	Execute a command	Whitelist of safe commands; blocklist for destructive ones
Tier 3 — Dangerous (require explicit confirmation)

Tool	What it does	Safety requirement
delete_file	Remove a file	Confirm + git check (is it tracked?)
run_shell_unsafe	Sudo, installs, destructive commands	Explicit confirm with preview
git_commit	Stage and commit	Show diff, confirm message
The hard part — edit_file
Most coding agents fail here. Writing a new file is easy. Editing one correctly is where you earn the product.

The pattern that works:

typescript
// Input from agent:
{
  path: "src/auth/login.ts",
  find: `  async function validateToken(token: string) {
    return jwt.verify(token, process.env.SECRET);
  }`,
  replace: `  async function validateToken(token: string): Promise<User | null> {
    try {
      const payload = jwt.verify(token, process.env.SECRET!) as JwtPayload;
      return await db.users.findOne({ id: payload.userId });
    } catch {
      return null;
    }
  }`
}
The tool finds the find block exactly in the file (with whitespace normalisation), replaces it with replace, and errors clearly if the block isn't found. The agent gets the error back and can adjust. No full-file rewrites. No guessing line numbers.

Three failure modes to handle:

Block not found → return error + first 50 lines of file so agent can recalibrate
Multiple matches → require more context (longer find block)
File changed since read → re-read and retry
Context management — the other hard part
The context window is finite. A real codebase is not. You need a strategy.

Project context (loaded at startup):

README.md (always)
Directory tree (max 3 levels, filtered by .gitignore)
package.json / requirements.txt / Cargo.toml (whatever's relevant)
Recent git log (last 5 commits)
Any rubycode.json project config
Task context (loaded per task):

Files explicitly mentioned in the task
Files found via search_code during planning
Test files related to touched source files
Smart truncation:

Files > 300 lines: show first 80 lines + table of contents (function signatures, class names) + last 20 lines
Agent can always read_file with a line range for the part it needs
Session memory:

Full tool call history for the current session
Trimmed when approaching context limit (keep first turn + last 6 tool exchanges)
Phases
Phase 1 — The working CLI agent (3–5 days)

Deliverable: ruby-code "fix the bug where users can't log in" works reliably on a real project.

What you build:

Agent loop (the TypeScript pattern above)
Tier 1 tools (all read-only)
edit_file and write_file
Basic run_shell (safe commands only)
Project context loader
CLI display (streaming tool calls to terminal)

Not yet: session memory, multi-agent, UI, git integration.

Phase 2 — Reliability + developer features (1 week)

edit_file hardened (the three failure modes)
run_tests — verify changes automatically
Git integration (status, diff, optional commit)
Session memory + --continue to resume
Interactive REPL mode (keep the agent "on")
Episode memory (remember what worked in past sessions — your AgentMesh pattern applied here)
rubycode.json project config
Phase 3 — Multi-agent (1–2 weeks)

This is where Ruby Diamond diverges from Claude Code and becomes something more.

CEO Agent (your term): orchestrates the task, decomposes, assigns to specialists
Coding Agent: the Phase 1/2 agent, specialised for implementation
Research Agent: reads docs, searches the web, summarises context
Review Agent: reads changes, identifies issues, suggests improvements
Each agent has its own tool subset and specialised system prompt
Orchestrator uses your AgentMesh pattern — episode memory, recursive learning, safeguards
Phase 4 — Ruby Diamond desktop (2–3 weeks)

Tauri shell wrapping the Phase 1–3 agent as a sidecar process
React frontend: Monaco editor, terminal panel, agent chat panel, file tree
Live streaming of agent activity to the UI
The architecture from your infographics — fully realised

With those three tools and the loop, you can test: "Add a console.log to the login function in src/auth.ts." If that works reliably, the rest is adding tools and hardening the core.

One decision before we write code
Where does this live? Two options:

Option A — Standalone repo (ruby-code on GitHub) Clean separation, independently installable via npm install -g ruby-code, becomes its own product. The right call if you want to publish it.

Option B — Inside your existing monorepo Faster start, shares utils with your other projects (AgentMesh, Hermes). Right call if you want to build fast and integrate later.

Given you're building toward Ruby Diamond as a product, Option A makes more sense — it's your own Claude Code, separately installable, part of the Ruby ecosystem.

What you have
The provider abstraction — the architecture change you asked for. The agent loop never imports Anthropic, OpenAI, or Google. It takes a LLMProvider interface and calls provider.stream(). Swap the model with --model.

Four providers, all working:

AnthropicProvider — native SDK, proper streaming with SMIL-style buffering
OpenAICompatibleProvider — covers OpenAI, xAI/Grok, OpenRouter, Ollama, LM Studio, any custom endpoint with one flag (--base-url)
GoogleProvider — Gemini via @google/generative-ai
factory.ts — auto-detects from model name: claude-* → Anthropic, grok-* → xAI, ollama/* → Ollama, anything else → OpenAI-compatible
Nine tools: read_file, list_dir, edit_file (three-tier fuzzy matching), write_file, search_code (ripgrep/grep), run_shell, run_tests (auto-detects Jest/Vitest/pytest/go/cargo), git_status, git_diff

Permission system: --readonly / default normal / --auto — dangerous ops need confirmation unless --auto

Diagnosed PATH configuration issue and formulated diagnostic steps
Diagnosed PATH configuration issue and formulated diagnostic steps
npm link worked but npm's global bin isn't in your PATH. Quick diagnosis:

bash
npm config get prefix
That'll show something like /home/dusanmilosavljevic/.npm-global or /usr. The binary is at <prefix>/bin/ruby-code. Add it to PATH permanently:

bash
# Check where it ended up
ls $(npm config get prefix)/bin/ruby-code

# Add to PATH (run this + add to ~/.bashrc)
export PATH="$(npm config get prefix)/bin:$PATH"
echo 'export PATH="'$(npm config get prefix)'/bin:$PATH"' >> ~/.bashrc
Then test:

bash
ruby-code -m mimo-v2.5-pro "review the agent loop in src/agent/loop.ts"
If you want to skip all that and just run it right now:

bash
node ~/ruby-code/dist/cli/index.js -m mimo-v2.5-pro "review the agent loop in src/agent/loop.ts"
Also check the compiled entry point has the shebang and execute bit:

bash
head -1 ~/ruby-code/dist/cli/index.js   # should say #!/usr/bin/env node
chmod +x ~/ruby-code/dist/cli/index.js
If the shebang is missing (TypeScript sometimes strips it), run this once:

bash
sed -i '1s/^/#!/usr/bin\/env node\n/' ~/ruby-code/dist/cli/index.js
Then npm link again and it'll work.

  📄 read_file  src/tools/tools.ts

  📄 read_file  src/cli/display.ts
  ✓ src/cli/display.ts (144 lines): (+145 lines) 1ms
Here's my review of src/agent/loop.ts:
---
## Overall Assessment
The loop is **well-structured and readable** — clean separation of concerns, good use of the streaming API, and a sensible permission system. That said, there are several issues ranging from correctness bugs to robustness gaps.
---
## 🔴 Bugs / Correctness Issues
### 1. Empty responseToolCalls never checked against stream exhaustion
**Line 72–74:** The done chunk fallback only copies tool calls if responseToolCalls.length === 0, but it checks chunk.response.toolCalls.length > 0. If the stream yields *partial* tool calls (e.g. some via tool_end but not all), you'll silently drop the ones from chunk.response. This is fragile — the two sources of truth can diverge.
**Recommendation:** After the stream loop, if finalResponse has tool calls, always reconcile with responseToolCalls (e.g. merge by id, or prefer one source consistently).
### 2. Stop condition is ambiguous when responseText is empty and no tool calls
**Line 82:** The condition !finalResponse || finalResponse.stopReason === 'done' || responseToolCalls.length === 0 will return success even if the model produced *nothing* — no text and no tool calls. This could happen if a provider streams a degenerate empty response. You'd silently succeed with summary: '(Task completed)' when the task likely wasn't addressed.
**Recommendation:** Add a guard: if responseText is empty AND responseToolCalls.length === 0 AND turns === 1, treat it as an error or retry.
### 3. Tool result errors aren't flagged as isError to the provider in a standard way
**Line 137:** When a tool succeeds, you push { id: call.id, content: result } without isError: false. That's fine since it's optional — but the real issue is on **line 117**: blocked tool calls push isError: true, which is correct. However, when executeTool itself throws, there's no catch. If the tool implementation throws an unhandled exception, the entire loop crashes.
**Recommendation:** Wrap executeTool in a try/catch:
typescript
try {
  const result = await executeTool(call.name, call.input, context.root);
  // ...
} catch (err) {
  display.toolResult(call.name, `Error: ${String(err)}`, Date.now() - startMs);
  toolResults.push({ id: call.id, content: `Error: ${String(err)}`, isError: true });
}

---
## 🟡 Robustness / Edge Cases
### 4. No abort/cancellation mechanism
There's no way to cancel a long-running stream or tool execution (e.g. user presses Ctrl+C during a 30s shell command). The while loop has no AbortSignal integration. The provider's stream() generator and executeTool both have no cancellation path.
**Recommendation:** Thread an AbortSignal through LoopOptions → provider.stream() → executeTool() and listen for SIGINT.
### 5. No token/context window management
The history array grows unboundedly. After many turns with large tool results (e.g. reading 300-line files), you'll blow past the model's context window. The loop will just hit the provider's error or limit stop reason unexpectedly.
**Recommendation:** Track approximate token count and truncate/summarize old tool results when approaching the context limit.
### 6. formatCallForConfirmation references delete_file but no such tool exists
**Line 156:** The confirmation formatter checks for delete_file, but looking at tools.ts and tools/index.ts, there is no delete_file tool registered. This is dead code. Not a bug per se, but it suggests a mismatch between planned and actual tool set.
---
## 🟢 Style / Minor
### 7. confirm import could be consolidated
**Lines 3–4:** Two separate imports from the same module:
typescript
import { PermissionSystem } from '../safety/permissions.js';
import { confirm } from '../safety/permissions.js';

Should be:
typescript
import { PermissionSystem, confirm } from '../safety/permissions.js';

### 8. The tool_input case is a no-op
**Line 62–63:** The tool_input case does nothing (with a comment saying so). This is fine for documentation, but consider whether you want to show partial tool input for long commands (e.g. a write_file with 200 lines of content) — it would improve perceived responsiveness.
### 9. maxLoopTurns default of 50 may be too high
For most tasks, 50 turns is excessive and risks burning through API credits if the model gets into a retry loop. Consider 25 as a safer default, with the --max-turns flag for power users.
---
## Summary Table
| # | Severity | Issue |
|---|----------|-------|
| 1 | 🔴 Bug | Tool call reconciliation between stream and done chunk is fragile |
| 2 | 🔴 Bug | Empty response silently succeeds |
| 3 | 🔴 Bug | No try/catch around executeTool — unhandled throw kills the loop |
| 4 | 🟡 Robustness | No abort signal / cancellation support |
| 5 | 🟡 Robustness | Unbounded history growth — no context window management |
| 6 | 🟡 Dead code | delete_file in confirmation formatter doesn't exist |
| 7 | 🟢 Style | Duplicate import lines |
| 8 | 🟢 Style | tool_input is a silent no-op |
| 9 | 🟢 Style | 50 max turns is aggressive as a default |
The architecture is solid. Issues #3 (missing try/catch) and #2 (empty response edge case) are the most likely to bite in production. Want me to fix any of these?

  ---

  ## Overall Assessment

  The loop is **well-structured and readable** — clean separation of concerns, good use of the streaming API, and a sensible permission system. That said, there are several issues ranging from correctness bugs to robustness gaps.

  ## 🔴 Bugs / Correctness Issues

  ### 2. Stop condition is ambiguous when responseText is empty and no tool calls

  **Recommendation:** Add a guard: if responseText is empty AND responseToolCalls.length === 0 AND turns === 1, treat it as an error or retry.

  ### 3. Tool result errors aren't flagged as isError to the provider in a standard way

  ---

  ## 🟡 Robustness / Edge Cases

  ### 4. No abort/cancellation mechanism
  There's no way to cancel a long-running stream or tool execution (e.g. user presses Ctrl+C during a 30s shell command). The while loop has no AbortSignal integration. The provider's stream() generator and executeTool both have no cancellation path.

  **Recommendation:** Thread an AbortSignal through LoopOptions → provider.stream() → executeTool() and listen for SIGINT.

7	Import merged	Single import { PermissionSystem, confirm } from '../safety/permissions.js'

May 31
Yes. Ollama is the easiest path on Fedora — one install, runs locally, no API key.

bash
# Install Ollama
curl -fsSL https://ollama.com/install.sh | sh

# Pull a coding model (pick one)
ollama pull qwen2.5-coder    # best for code, 7B — fast on most hardware
ollama pull codellama        # Meta's coding model
ollama pull llama3.2         # general purpose, good all-rounder
ollama pull deepseek-coder   # strong on reasoning + code

# Ollama runs at localhost:11434 by default — verify it's up
ollama list
Then just use the ollama/ prefix with ruby-code:

bash
ruby-code -m ollama/qwen2.5-coder "review the agent loop"
ruby-code -m ollama/llama3.2 "what does this project do?"
ruby-code -m ollama/deepseek-coder "fix the TypeScript errors"
No API key needed. The provider factory already handles it — ollama/* routes to OpenAICompatibleProvider pointing at http://localhost:11434/v1 automatically.

Check your hardware first — the model has to fit in RAM (or VRAM if you have a GPU):

Model	RAM needed	Good for
qwen2.5-coder:7b	~6 GB	Coding tasks, fast
llama3.2:3b	~3 GB	Quick tasks, very fast
llama3.2:8b	~6 GB	General, solid quality
codellama:13b	~10 GB	Deeper code understanding
qwen2.5-coder:32b	~22 GB	Near-cloud quality
Check what you have:

bash
free -h          # RAM
nvidia-smi       # GPU VRAM (if Nvidia)
If Ollama isn't your preference, LM Studio also works — it exposes the same OpenAI-compatible endpoint at localhost:1234:

bash
ruby-code -m local/any-model --base-url http://localhost:1234/v1 "task"

Architected persistent web client to replace one-shot CLI tool
Architected persistent web client to replace one-shot CLI tool
Right. What we built is a hammer — you swing it once. What you want is a workshop — you sit in it and work.

The difference is architecture:

ruby-code "fix the bug"     ← runs, exits. Tool.

Claude Code / Cursor        ← persistent session, you live in it,
                              conversation memory, UI that stays open.
Three options, fastest to most complete:

A — Terminal UI (like Claude Code exactly) — split pane TUI in the terminal, persistent REPL, streaming. Build time: today.

B — Web client (browser as UI, localhost server) — chat panel, file tree, live diffs, model switcher. Looks like a product. Build time: today.

C — Tauri desktop app (from your Ruby Diamond infographic) — native app, Monaco editor, full shell. Build time: 1–2 weeks.

Given you want something working today, B is the right call — a local web client. Browser stays open, conversation is persistent, you see everything streaming in real time, full Ruby aesthetic. Same agent loop underneath, just with a proper UI. And it becomes the frontend for the Tauri app later — same React code, just wrapped in a native shell.

Building it now:

ruby-code serve
→ server starts on localhost:7337
→ browser opens automatically
→ persistent conversation with your codebase

Why you got the 401: your machine was running the first build we did — before the Xiaomi changes. ruby-code serve
Also worth noting — your npm global prefix is .hermes/node/bin/, which means npm link is putting binaries inside your Hermes project's Node installation. That's fine and it works, just a little unusual. If you ever want to separate them:

bash
npm config set prefix ~/.npm-global
echo 'export PATH="$HOME/.npm-global/bin:$PATH"' >> ~/.bashrc
source ~/.bashrc
cd ~/ruby-code && npm link

Permission issue on the linked binary itself. Fix it directly:

bash

If that still fails (sometimes npm link creates a symlink and chmod doesn't follow through):

bash

ls -la ~/.hermes/node/bin/ruby-code

chmod +x ~/ruby-code/dist/cli/index.js

# Re-link

# Try again

If npm link keeps having permission issues, skip it entirely and just run directly:

bash
node ~/ruby-code/dist/cli/index.js serve
Or add a permanent alias that bypasses the permission problem:

bash
echo 'alias ruby-code="node /home/dusanmilosavljevic/ruby-code/dist/cli/index.js"' >> ~/.bashrc
source ~/.bashrc
ruby-code serve
The alias approach is actually cleaner on Fedora when npm's global bin has permission quirks — it always runs directly via node with no binary wrapper.

May 31

Catalogued app capabilities and compiled practical usage examples
Catalogued app capabilities and compiled practical usage examples
What ruby-code can do

Read & understand
  ├── reads any file in your project (with line ranges)
  ├── builds a directory tree and understands the structure
  ├── searches across the whole codebase with regex
  ├── reads git status and diffs
  └── auto-detects your language, framework, test runner

Write & change
  ├── edits files with targeted find/replace (not full rewrites)
  ├── creates new files and directories
  ├── runs shell commands (build, lint, format, install)
  └── runs your test suite and reads the results

Work with any model
  ├── claude-opus-4-5      (Anthropic)
  ├── mimo-v2.5-pro        (Xiaomi — your token plan)
  ├── gpt-4o               (OpenAI)
  ├── gemini-2.5-pro       (Google)
  ├── grok-beta            (xAI)
  ├── ollama/llama3.2      (local, no internet)
  └── openrouter/*         (any model via OpenRouter)

Three modes
  ├── ruby-code "task"        one shot, exits when done
  ├── ruby-code --interactive stay open, multi-turn conversation
  └── ruby-code serve         browser UI at localhost:7337
Examples — real commands

bash
# ── Understand ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────
ruby-code "explain what this codebase does and how it's structured"
ruby-code "what does the RecursiveLearner in src/memory/episodes.ts do"
ruby-code "find all places where we call the Anthropic API"
ruby-code "what tests exist for the agent loop"

# ── Fix bugs ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
ruby-code "fix the 401 error when using the Xiaomi provider"
ruby-code "the tests are failing — find out why and fix it"
ruby-code "there's a memory leak somewhere in the session manager"

# ── Add features ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────
ruby-code "add a --max-turns flag to the CLI"
ruby-code "add a delete_file tool to the tool registry"
ruby-code "add retry logic with exponential backoff to the provider"

# ── Refactor ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
ruby-code "rename LoopOptions to AgentOptions everywhere"
ruby-code "split tools.ts into one file per tool"
ruby-code "convert the callbacks in display.ts to async/await"

# ── Tests ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
ruby-code "write unit tests for the edit_file tool"
ruby-code "add a test that verifies the empty response guard works"
ruby-code "run the tests and fix anything that's failing"

# ── Code review ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────
ruby-code "review src/agent/loop.ts for bugs and robustness issues"
ruby-code "check the providers for security issues"
ruby-code "is there anything in this codebase I should fix before shipping"

# ── Documentation ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
ruby-code "add JSDoc comments to all exported functions in src/tools/"
ruby-code "update the README to reflect the current CLI flags"
ruby-code "write a CONTRIBUTING.md for this project"
Interactive REPL — multi-turn workflow

bash
ruby-code --interactive --cwd ~/agentmesh

  ▸ what does the harness optimizer do
  [reads files, explains it]

  ▸ add error handling to the worst case you just described
  [edits the file]

  ▸ now write a test for that
  [creates test file]

  ▸ run the tests
  [runs pytest, shows output]

  ▸ :model mimo-v2.5     ← switch to a faster model mid-session
  ▸ explain what we just did in plain English
Web client — ruby-code serve

bash
ruby-code serve --cwd ~/your-project
# → opens localhost:7337 in browser
# → persistent chat, streaming output
# → file tree on the left
# → every tool call shown as it happens
# → switch model from the dropdown
# → "New chat" to reset, conversation persists until then
Permission modes

bash
# Default — writes shown, destructive ops need confirmation
ruby-code "refactor the auth module"

# Read-only — safe for exploration, can't touch files
ruby-code --readonly "explain the whole codebase to me"

# Auto — no confirmations, runs everything (good for CI or trusted tasks)
ruby-code --auto "fix all TypeScript errors and run the build"

bash
cd ~/ruby-code

Do exactly these steps in order:

1. Run: npm install --save express ws

3. Create the file src/server/session.ts with this exact content:
---
import type { HistoryMessage } from '../providers/types.js';
export interface SessionMessage {
  id: string; role: string; content: string;
  toolName?: string; toolInput?: Record<string, unknown>;
  timestamp: number; turns?: number; toolCount?: number;
}
export class Session {
  private history: HistoryMessage[] = [];
  private display: SessionMessage[] = [];
  private n = 0;
  getHistory() { return [...this.history]; }
  getDisplay() { return [...this.display]; }
  addUser(content: string) { this.history.push({role:'user',content}); this.display.push({id:'m'+(++this.n),role:'user',content,timestamp:Date.now()}); }
  addAssistant(content: string, turns=0, toolCount=0) { this.display.push({id:'m'+(++this.n),role:'assistant',content,timestamp:Date.now(),turns,toolCount}); }
  addTool(toolName: string, toolInput: Record<string,unknown>, content: string) { this.display.push({id:'m'+(++this.n),role:'tool_call',toolName,toolInput,content,timestamp:Date.now()}); }
  reset() { this.history=[]; this.display=[]; }
}

import * as http from 'http';
import express from 'express';
import { WebSocketServer, WebSocket } from 'ws';
import { createProvider, KNOWN_MODELS } from '../providers/factory.js';
import { loadProjectContext } from '../agent/context.js';
import { runAgentLoop } from '../agent/loop.js';
import { PermissionSystem } from '../safety/permissions.js';
import { Session } from './session.js';
import type { Display } from '../cli/display.js';
import type { ProviderConfig } from '../providers/types.js';

export interface ServeOptions {
  port: number; cwd: string; model: string;
  apiKey?: string; baseUrl?: string; open: boolean;
}

export async function startServer(opts: ServeOptions): Promise<void> {
  const app = express();
  const server = http.createServer(app);
  const wss = new WebSocketServer({ server });
  app.use(express.json());
  const ctx = await loadProjectContext(opts.cwd);
  const session = new Session();
  console.log('\n  Ruby Code — web client');
  console.log('  Project : ' + ctx.name + ' · ' + ctx.language);
  console.log('  Model   : ' + opts.model);
  console.log('  URL     : http://localhost:' + opts.port + '\n');
  app.get('/', (_req, res) => { res.setHeader('Content-Type','text/html'); res.send('<html><body style=\"background:#0e0a06;color:#ede0cc;font-family:system-ui;display:flex;flex-direction:column;height:100vh;margin:0\"><div style=\"background:#150e08;border-bottom:1px solid #2c1e14;padding:14px 20px;font-size:16px\"><em style=\"color:#cc785c;font-style:italic\">Ruby</em> Code — ' + ctx.name + '</div><div id=\"ch\" style=\"flex:1;overflow-y:auto;padding:20px;display:flex;flex-direction:column;gap:10px\"></div><div style=\"background:#150e08;border-top:1px solid #2c1e14;padding:14px 20px;display:flex;gap:10px\"><input id=\"inp\" style=\"flex:1;background:#1c1208;border:1px solid #3a2818;border-radius:8px;padding:12px 16px;color:#ede0cc;font-size:14px;outline:none\" placeholder=\"Ask anything about your code…\" onkeydown=\"if(event.key===&quot;Enter&quot;)go()\"/><button onclick=\"go()\" style=\"background:#cc785c;color:#fff;border:none;border-radius:8px;padding:12px 20px;cursor:pointer;font-size:14px\">Send</button></div><script>const ch=document.getElementById(&quot;ch&quot;),inp=document.getElementById(&quot;inp&quot;);let ws=new WebSocket(&quot;ws://&quot;+location.host),cur=null,curT=&quot;&quot;;ws.onmessage=e=>{const d=JSON.parse(e.data);if(d.type===&quot;text&quot;){if(!cur){cur=mk();ch.appendChild(cur);}curT+=d.text;cur.innerHTML=esc(curT)+&quot;<span style=display:inline-block;width:8px;height:14px;background:#cc785c;margin-left:2px;animation:b 1s steps(1) infinite></span>&quot;;sc();}if(d.type===&quot;text_end&quot;||d.type===&quot;done&quot;){if(cur){cur.innerHTML=esc(curT);cur=null;curT=&quot;&quot;;}sc();}if(d.type===&quot;tool_call&quot;){const e=mk(&quot;#1c1208&quot;,&quot;11px&quot;,&quot;var(--mono)&quot;);e.innerHTML=&quot;🔧 &quot;+d.name+&quot; &quot;+esc(JSON.stringify(d.input).slice(0,80));ch.appendChild(e);sc();}};function go(){const t=inp.value.trim();if(!t)return;const u=mk(&quot;#efe0d4&quot;);u.textContent=t;ch.appendChild(u);inp.value=&quot;&quot;;ws.send(JSON.stringify({type:&quot;task&quot;,task:t}));sc();}function mk(bg,fs,ff){const e=document.createElement(&quot;div&quot;);e.style.cssText=&quot;background:&quot;+(bg||&quot;#1c1208&quot;)+&quot;;border-radius:8px;padding:12px 16px;line-height:1.6;font-size:&quot;+(fs||&quot;14px&quot;)+&quot;;white-space:pre-wrap&quot;;if(ff)e.style.fontFamily=ff;return e;}function esc(s){return String(s).replace(/&/g,&quot;&amp;&quot;).replace(/</g,&quot;&lt;&quot;).replace(/>/g,&quot;&gt;&quot;);}function sc(){ch.scrollTop=ch.scrollHeight;}<\/script><style>@keyframes b{0%,100%{opacity:1}50%{opacity:0}}</style></body></html>'); });
  app.get('/api/history', (_req, res) => res.json(session.getDisplay()));
  app.get('/api/project', (_req, res) => res.json({ name: ctx.name, language: ctx.language, model: opts.model, models: KNOWN_MODELS }));
  app.post('/api/reset', (_req, res) => { session.reset(); res.json({ ok: true }); });
  wss.on('connection', (ws) => {
    ws.on('message', async (raw) => {
      let msg: { type: string; task?: string; model?: string };
      try { msg = JSON.parse(raw.toString()); } catch { return; }
      if (msg.type === 'task' && msg.task) await runTask(ws, msg.task, msg.model ?? opts.model);
      if (msg.type === 'reset') { session.reset(); }
    });
  });
  async function runTask(ws: WebSocket, task: string, model: string): Promise<void> {
    session.addUser(task);
    let provider;
    try { provider = createProvider({ model, apiKey: opts.apiKey, baseUrl: opts.baseUrl } as ProviderConfig); }
    catch (e) { ws.send(JSON.stringify({ type: 'error', message: String(e) })); return; }
    const display: Display = {
      agentThinking: () => {},
      streamText: (t) => ws.send(JSON.stringify({ type: 'text', text: t })),
      streamEnd: () => ws.send(JSON.stringify({ type: 'text_end' })),
      toolStart: () => {},
      toolCall: (name, input) => ws.send(JSON.stringify({ type: 'tool_call', name, input })),
      toolResult: (name, result, ms) => ws.send(JSON.stringify({ type: 'tool_result', name, result, ms })),
      toolBlocked: (name, reason) => ws.send(JSON.stringify({ type: 'tool_blocked', name, reason })),
      warning: (msg) => ws.send(JSON.stringify({ type: 'warning', message: msg })),
      success: () => {},
      error: (msg) => ws.send(JSON.stringify({ type: 'error', message: msg })),
      header: () => {},
      summary: (text, turns, toolCount) => ws.send(JSON.stringify({ type: 'done', text, turns, toolCount, success: true })),
    };
    const result = await runAgentLoop({ provider, task, context: ctx, permissions: new PermissionSystem('normal'), display });
    session.addAssistant(result.summary, result.turns, result.toolCallCount);
  }
  server.listen(opts.port, () => {
    if (opts.open) { try { require('child_process').exec('xdg-open http://localhost:' + opts.port); } catch {} }
    console.log('  Ready at http://localhost:' + opts.port + '  (Ctrl+C to stop)\n');
  });
}

  });
}

python3 << 'EOF'
content = open('/dev/stdin').read() if False else """
import * as http from 'http';
import express from 'express';
import { WebSocketServer, WebSocket } from 'ws';
import { createProvider, KNOWN_MODELS } from '../providers/factory.js';
import { loadProjectContext } from '../agent/context.js';
import { runAgentLoop } from '../agent/loop.js';
import { PermissionSystem } from '../safety/permissions.js';
import { Session } from './session.js';
import type { Display } from '../cli/display.js';
import type { ProviderConfig } from '../providers/types.js';

export interface ServeOptions {
  port: number; cwd: string; model: string;
  apiKey?: string; baseUrl?: string; open: boolean;
}

export async function startServer(opts: ServeOptions): Promise<void> {
  const app = express();
  const server = http.createServer(app);
  const wss = new WebSocketServer({ server });
  app.use(express.json());
  const ctx = await loadProjectContext(opts.cwd);
  const session = new Session();
  console.log('\\n  Ruby Code — web client');
  console.log('  Project : ' + ctx.name + ' · ' + ctx.language);
  console.log('  Model   : ' + opts.model);
  console.log('  URL     : http://localhost:' + opts.port + '\\n');
  app.get('/', (_req, res) => { res.setHeader('Content-Type','text/html'); res.send(buildUI(ctx.name, opts.model)); });
  app.get('/api/history', (_req, res) => res.json(session.getDisplay()));
  app.get('/api/project', (_req, res) => res.json({ name: ctx.name, language: ctx.language, model: opts.model, models: KNOWN_MODELS }));
  app.post('/api/reset', (_req, res) => { session.reset(); res.json({ ok: true }); });
  wss.on('connection', (ws) => {
    send(ws, { type: 'connected' });
    ws.on('message', async (raw) => {
      let msg: { type: string; task?: string; model?: string };
      try { msg = JSON.parse(raw.toString()); } catch { return; }
      if (msg.type === 'task' && msg.task) await runTask(ws, msg.task, msg.model ?? opts.model);
      if (msg.type === 'reset') { session.reset(); send(ws, { type: 'reset_ok' }); }
    });
  });
  async function runTask(ws: WebSocket, task: string, model: string): Promise<void> {
    session.addUser(task);
    let provider;
    try { provider = createProvider({ model, apiKey: opts.apiKey, baseUrl: opts.baseUrl } as ProviderConfig); }
    catch (e) { send(ws, { type: 'error', message: String(e) }); return; }
    const display: Display = {
      agentThinking: () => send(ws, { type: 'thinking' }),
      streamText: (t) => send(ws, { type: 'text', text: t }),
      streamEnd: () => send(ws, { type: 'text_end' }),
      toolStart: () => {},
      toolCall: (name, input) => send(ws, { type: 'tool_call', name, input }),
      toolResult: (name, result, ms) => send(ws, { type: 'tool_result', name, result, ms }),
      toolBlocked: (name, reason) => send(ws, { type: 'tool_blocked', name, reason }),
      warning: (msg) => send(ws, { type: 'warning', message: msg }),
      success: () => {},
      error: (msg) => send(ws, { type: 'error', message: msg }),
      header: () => {},
      summary: (text, turns, toolCount) => send(ws, { type: 'done', text, turns, toolCount, success: true }),
    };
    const result = await runAgentLoop({ provider, task, context: ctx, permissions: new PermissionSystem('normal'), display });
    session.addAssistant(result.summary, result.turns, result.toolCallCount);
    if (!result.success) send(ws, { type: 'done', success: false, text: result.summary, turns: result.turns, toolCount: result.toolCallCount });
  }
  server.listen(opts.port, () => {
    if (opts.open) { try { require('child_process').exec('xdg-open http://localhost:' + opts.port); } catch {} }
    console.log('  Ready at http://localhost:' + opts.port + '  (Ctrl+C to stop)\\n');
  });
}

function send(ws: WebSocket, data: object): void {
  if (ws.readyState === WebSocket.OPEN) ws.send(JSON.stringify(data));
}

function buildUI(project: string, defaultModel: string): string {
  const opts = KNOWN_MODELS.map(m => '<option value="' + m.id + '">' + m.provider + ' — ' + m.name + '</option>').join('');
  return '<!doctype html><html><head><meta charset="utf-8"/><title>' + project + ' — Ruby Code</title>'
    + '<style>'
    + ':root{--bg:#0e0a06;--s:#1c1208;--ink:#ede0cc;--inks:#c8b5a0;--m:#8a7768;--f:#4e3d30;--c:#cc785c;--cd:#b15439;--g:#5a9e6e;--l:#2c1e14;--l2:#3a2818}'
    + '*{box-sizing:border-box;margin:0;padding:0}'
    + 'html,body{height:100%;background:var(--bg);color:var(--ink);font-family:system-ui,sans-serif;font-size:14px;overflow:hidden}'
    + '.app{display:grid;grid-template-rows:52px 1fr 76px;height:100vh}'
    + '.bar{background:#150e08;border-bottom:1px solid var(--l2);display:flex;align-items:center;gap:12px;padding:0 20px}'
    + '.logo{font-family:Georgia,serif;font-size:17px;font-weight:600}.logo em{font-style:italic;color:var(--c)}'
    + '.dot{width:7px;height:7px;border-radius:50%;background:var(--f)}'
    + '.dot.on{background:var(--g);box-shadow:0 0 0 3px rgba(90,158,110,.2)}'
    + 'select{margin-left:auto;background:var(--s);border:1px solid var(--l2);color:var(--inks);border-radius:6px;padding:6px 10px;font-size:11px}'
    + '.btn-r{font-size:11px;color:var(--m);background:transparent;border:1px solid var(--l2);border-radius:5px;padding:5px 10px;cursor:pointer}'
    + '.btn-r:hover{border-color:var(--c);color:var(--c)}'
    + '.chat{overflow-y:auto;padding:20px;display:flex;flex-direction:column;gap:10px}'
    + '.ibar{background:#150e08;border-top:1px solid var(--l2);padding:14px 18px;display:flex;gap:10px;align-items:flex-end}'
    + '.iw{flex:1;background:var(--s);border:1px solid var(--l2);border-radius:12px;display:flex;align-items:flex-end;padding:8px 12px;gap:10px}'
    + '.iw:focus-within{border-color:var(--c)}'
    + '#inp{flex:1;background:transparent;border:none;outline:none;color:var(--ink);font-size:14px;resize:none;max-height:130px;line-height:1.5;padding:4px 0}'
    + '#inp::placeholder{color:var(--f)}'

    + 'if(d.type==="text_end"||d.type==="done"){fn();if(d.type==="done")idle();sc();return;}'
    + 'if(d.type==="tool_call"){rt();var e=mk("div","mt");e.innerHTML=\'<div class="tn">\'+ic(d.name)+" "+d.name+\'</div><div class="ti">\'+ex(si(d.name,d.input))+\'</div><div class="tr">running\u2026</div>\';ch.appendChild(e);tEl=e;sc();return;}'

    + 'function idle(){busy=false;sb.disabled=false;}'
    + 'function go(){var t=inp.value.trim();if(!t||busy)return;var e=mk("div","mu");e.innerHTML=\'<div class="b">\'+ex(t)+"</div>";ch.appendChild(e);inp.value="";ar();busy=true;sb.disabled=true;ws.send(JSON.stringify({type:"task",task:t,model:ms.value}));sc();}'

    + 'sb.addEventListener("click",go);'
    + 'inp.addEventListener("keydown",function(e){if(e.key==="Enter"&&!e.shiftKey){e.preventDefault();go();}});'
    + 'inp.addEventListener("input",ar);'
    + 'document.getElementById("btnR").addEventListener("click",function(){fetch("/api/reset",{method:"POST"});ws.send(JSON.stringify({type:"reset"}));});'
    + 'conn();'
    + '<\/script></body></html>';

    + 'function hv(d){'
    + 'if(d.type==="thinking"){rt();var e=mk("div","tk");e.id="thi";e.textContent="thinking\u2026";ch.appendChild(e);sc();return;}'
